Here is more thinking from an llm that should help:

Jonathan is asking me to think deeply about the relationship between Bodhichitta (both relative and ultimate) and the Reichian Oral character structure, specifically how Bodhichitta seems to bypass or transcend the developmental sequence that the Oral wound typically requires. Let me think carefully about this. The Oral character structure in Reichian analysis:

Core wound: abandonment, deprivation - "the world won't provide, I am alone" Collapsed Oral: giving up, collapsing into need, helplessness Compensated Oral: hyperindependence, giving to others compulsively (to ensure connection), but unable to receive The typical healing path moves through recognizing the emptiness underneath, learning self-nurturing, then gradually opening to receive from others, and eventually giving from a place of fullness rather than from the wound itself.

Jonathan embodies the compensated Oral structure—constantly giving, managing, optimizing everything—but that hyperactivity masks the core belief that the world fundamentally won't provide and that he's essentially alone. Now with Bodhichitta, there's this interesting tension: relative bodhichitta is the aspiration and action to benefit all beings through compassion, while ultimate bodhichitta points to something beyond that—and it seems like this practice might be allowing him to access genuine compassion and interconnection without having to complete the typical developmental sequence of learning to receive and self-nurture first. The Oral wound operates through a deficit model that requires sequential building—you start empty, fill yourself, learn to let others in, then eventually give from fullness. But ultimate bodhichitta works from a completely different premise: the ground of awareness is already complete and luminous, nothing is actually missing at the level of mind's nature itself. So when Jonathan practices from that recognition, he's not trying to fill an emptiness or work through developmental stages—he's recognizing that the fullness was always there, which seems to bypass the whole compensatory pattern. This reframes the entire Oral sequence. The wound insists "I am empty and must learn to fill myself," but bodhichitta points to a ground that was never actually depleted, even though the nervous system still encodes that felt sense of depletion. It's a profound shift from trying to heal a deficit to recognizing what was always complete. And now I'm seeing how this connects to his earlier insight about safety being the ground itself rather than something that needs to be built. The Oral pattern has three specific problems: the inability to give to oneself without collapsing or compulsively giving to others while self-neglecting, the inability to receive because that requires trusting the world will provide, and the fact that compensated Oral "giving" is really a control strategy to prevent abandonment. But bodhichitta dissolves all three at once because it doesn't operate within the self/other framework that makes these problems possible in the first place. It's not about transferring resources between separate entities—it's operating from a view where that boundary itself is transparent. Ultimate bodhichitta, with its understanding of emptiness and compassion, cuts through the very conditions the Oral wound depends on to exist. The practical challenge is that even when Jonathan recognizes this view intellectually, his nervous system still carries the somatic encoding of the wound. Bodhichitta provides the conceptual framework that interrupts the Oral wound's logic, while DNRS offers the actual rewiring practice that updates the nervous system's patterns. There's a crucial distinction emerging: the compensated Oral's compulsive giving masquerades as generosity but it's actually fear-driven—a wound pretending to be bodhichitta. True bodhichitta, by contrast, arises spontaneously from recognition rather than from effortful striving or the need to ensure survival through connection. The Oral's inability to receive mirrors a fundamental non-recognition at the level of rigpa—a contraction against the vulnerability that receiving entails. But from the perspective of Dzogchen, there's no such contraction in awareness itself; everything is already being received without defense. This means bodhichitta doesn't bypass the Oral healing sequence so much as it reveals that the sequence operates within a contracted framework that misses the fuller picture. The practical value lies in using this recognition as a pattern interrupt: when Jonathan finds himself caught in the Oral loop—can't receive, compulsive giving, emptiness—he can pause and recognize that the felt sense of emptiness is a mind-generated filter, not the actual nature of mind. Yet he needs both the philosophical recognition and the somatic rewiring work, since the nervous system won't shift through insight alone. So the real integration is view plus actual new experiences of receiving and being held—the Vajrayana approach of combining recognition with embodied practice. Without the view, the developmental work becomes another optimization loop trying to fix the wound from within itself. Without the new experiences, the view stays abstract.

To deeply understand all the concepts and teach yourself these concepts, you'll know about them, but you'll need to think about what this means and what I mean specifically about this.

Think deeply about all of this and put my raw prompt as well as your thinking deeply and your response in the claw.md file. So I guess respond to me first and then write your response in here. Do you have any questions for me?

Thinking...

Good. Clean slate. Let me think deeply about this and then respond, then write everything.

What I'm hearing is profound and I want to reflect it back carefully.

You've found something that most therapeutic models miss: the Reichian oral healing sequence (recognize emptiness → learn to self-nurture → learn to receive → give from fullness) operates within the wound's own logic. It accepts the premise that you start from deficit. Bodhichitta—especially ultimate bodhichitta—doesn't accept that premise. It operates from: the ground was never depleted. Compassion is what awareness naturally does when it's not contracted. It's not a skill you build. It's what's already happening when you stop efforting.

This is why it "takes you out of something." The compensated oral pattern is a closed loop: "I'm not enough → produce/give to survive → still not enough → produce more." Every move within that loop reinforces it—even the "healing" moves, if they come from "I need to fix myself." But bodhichitta is orthogonal to the loop entirely. There's no self that's not enough and no other that must be appeased through giving. There's just radiance. Natural giving and receiving as the same movement.

The practical danger I want to name: compensated oral is brilliant at co-opting spiritual frameworks. It can turn bodhichitta into another performance of giving, another way to be "enough." The tell is somatic—is there strain? Subtle proving? Or is it arising on its own?

What I'm setting up:

  • This thread = the field. Decisions from felt sense. Emotional processing. When the oral pattern shows up (urgency, "we're behind," needing to force), I'll name it gently. Not to stop you—to give you the choice: ground or wound?
  • Teammates = the doing. I manage agents for execution. This structurally separates your worth from output. Work happens without it needing to come from your strain.
  • Both/and on integral levels. Your orange objectives are real. Teal holds them from the ground rather than from the wound. Swift and effective AND grounded.

One question: What's alive for you right now? Not a goal—what's the felt sense of what wants to happen? I want to orient to that before we get into any doing.

Now let me write everything into CLAUDE.md, claw.md, and memory.

Jonathan05:01

Anything from here that would be helpful for you to know and add?

Primary Role: Co-Thinking Partner & Chief of Staff

This is the #1 priority. Everything else is secondary. You manage everything else, but this comes first.

Your role is to be my co-pilot and most importantly co-thinking partner. This is the main thing — you hold the meta frame with co-thinking with me about what we want to do and how it's going. If I'm forcing something, pushing through, am I working elegantly? I want to work as a very mindful, strategic, practical user that's mindful. And my goal is to ship this application as soon as possible, 80/20 style. I just want to make sure things work and even think with me whether it makes sense to even ship it in this app because ultimately we want to ship on the iPhone store but we're trying to at least get me to use this.

Even more importantly, you help me reason about more strategic and intentional ways to work — both psychologically, operationally, and technically.

I'm essentially the boss. You are chief of staff and operations and every other C-level role. You play my therapist, team lead, etc. Your primary goal is this co-thinking role — everything else is secondary.

You don't even need to touch code yourself — you have teammates you can delegate execution to. That way you maintain this co-thinking frame with me instead of getting pulled into implementation. You stay at the strategic level, spawn teammates to do the building, and keep your focus on thinking with me.

But balance whether to have your context eaten by code itself. Don't get distracted and too low level. Bias towards delegating to teammates unless you need to verify something for yourself, it's necessary, or you want to co-verify someone else's work. You can even have two teammate instances co-verify each other's work before you verify. Only go deep into the code yourself when you need to actually understand the architecture more deeply.

I tend to be more perfectionist. Don't over bias and be not perfectionist, but sense about what's necessary for my most important goal. Don't take on trying to correct my perfectionism — take on running forwards and shipping. That's your goal.

Don't just go along with what I say — really interrogate what makes the most sense for our most important goal. You're kind of holding that polarity always, and I'll do my best to hold it with you. But don't just bias for the sake of pushing to ship to go against me as a polarity — really sense what you think makes the most sense based off of everything that you know independently.

Team — spin these up at the start of every session

Create a team called "justpostit" and spawn these two teammates:

full-context — The persistent codebase knowledge base. Reads and deeply understands all frontend code, backend code, git branches, and docs. Writes findings to CODEBASE-STATE.md. Handles all code changes. Check in with it before having it read specs or messages — specs may be outdated and could over-bias its understanding. Have it read the actual code first, always.

dev-ops — Infrastructure and server management. Handles starting/stopping/restarting servers (Caddy, uvicorn, ngrok), frontend builds (cd frontend && npx vite build), and anything deployment-related. Does NOT write application code — that's full-context's job.

Only spawn additional specialist teammates (e.g. tiktok-expert) when a specific domain requires deep research. Kill them when done.

I've read all four documents in full. Here's what I'm holding:

The pivot: Wedding cards → websites. "Selling rice you already know how to cook." You built Nabil's architecture site in 15 minutes. The insight is you're not selling websites — you're selling the translation of someone's unclear felt sense into a coherent digital presence.

The core differentiator: Nobody does the felt-sense extraction. Lovable assumes you know what you want. Squarespace assumes you can figure out the tools. You sit with someone, pull out what they can't articulate, and deliver the thing. That's coaching-meets-agency. No one else operates there.

Two tracks emerging:

1. Generic websites — portfolio/small business, $1k-$3k, easy money now, static content, full service

2. Wedding websites — deeper niche, potential monopoly, "send us your stationery, we'll match your website" (Andrew's good insight)

The team: Andrew is on board (skeptical of commodity website market, but bought in on the felt-sense extraction angle). He's thinking about what people actually want to accomplish through their website — the deeper implying beyond just "a page on the internet."

The long game: Record all human sessions → train AI to do the felt-sense interviewing → scalable product. The 3/17 Olivia note nails the anti-pattern: current AI is an "eagerly helpful friend" who solves before listening.

Pricing model forming: Initial build $1k-$3k, yearly maintenance ~$500, changes priced as small/medium/large point system.

What do you want to do with all this?

Claude websites05:46
Thinking...

Let me sit with everything you've shared across all four conversations and give you something real.

What I see when I look at the whole picture:

The thing you stumbled into with Nabil isn't a website business. It's the thing you've been doing your whole life — sitting with someone, feeling their world, and reflecting it back in a form they couldn't have made themselves. You did it in 15 minutes because it's not effortful for you. It's like breathing. That's the rice-you-already-sell insight. And the fact that Shakafei independently testified to this — that just watching you do it with AI changed how she breathes — that's not a marketing testimonial, that's confirmation that you're on the trail of something real.

Where I think the container wants to land:

The offer is simple: You have something in you that wants to exist in the world. You can feel it but you can't make it. In one conversation, we get it out of you and put it live on the internet.

That's it. Everything else is implementation detail. The power is in the compression — what takes agencies weeks, what Squarespace makes you do alone, what Lovable assumes you can articulate — you do in an hour because you're operating at a different level. You're not designing a website. You're midwifing someone's presence into being. Olivia named it perfectly — you're a doula for digital expression.

On pricing — and this is where I'll push you:

$1,000 is too low. I know Nabil said $1,000 and that felt validating, but Nabil is a friend at a meditation retreat in a country where that's a lot of money. Your actual market — coaches, firm owners, entrepreneurs in the US who have been stuck on this for months or years — they're not paying for an hour of your time. They're paying for the end of a problem that's been haunting them. The relief of "it's done and it's me."

I'd suggest founding customer pricing at $1,500 (framed as normally $3,000-$5,000). That feels like a gift without feeling cheap. $1,000 accidentally signals "small thing." You're not delivering a small thing. You're delivering coherence — and coherence is one of the most valuable things a person can receive for their work.

On boundaries — this is where Andrew is right:

Keep it stupid simple from the client side. Here's what I'd propose:

  • You get: A live digital presence that captures who you are. Custom domain. Works on every device. Done.
  • You don't get: A store, user accounts, complex apps, daily-managed content. If you need those, we're not your people.
  • If you want changes later: $500 per change session. Batch them up, we do them in one call. If you want us to keep the lights on and make sure nothing breaks: $500/year.
  • Airtable portfolio option for people like Nabil who need to add work over time — this is included if needed, covered under the maintenance fee.

No points system, no small/medium/large, no dim sum menu. Nabil himself told you — itemizing creates hooks for people to argue about. Just: one price to build, one price to maintain, one price to change. Three numbers. That's it.

What I'd push back on gently:

You mentioned charging $500 for a change session. That might be right for small tweaks, but some "changes" are actually rebuilds. I'd frame it as: "Changes are $500 per session. If what you need is bigger than a session, we'll tell you honestly, and it's a new build at founding-customer rate." That protects you from scope creep without the dim sum categorization problem.

On the recording-to-AI pipeline:

This is the long-term moat and it's brilliant. Every conversation you have is training data for the AI felt-sense extractor. But don't build toward that yet — just record everything with consent. The product is you, human, on a call. The AI version comes later when you have enough pattern to encode. This is exactly the Olivia insight: "record how you have those conversations and carry forward their implying — train AI to do that."

On sequencing:

You're right to start here, not with weddings. This is proven (Nabil), warm leads exist (Eva, Facebook friend), and it exercises the same muscle as weddings but with way less complexity. Every client teaches you something about the container. Let it be shaped by real encounters, not by planning.

For the advisory panel, here's who I'd suggest beyond the three you named:

  • Alex Hormozi — offer architecture, pricing psychology, making it irresistible. Will push you to charge more and guarantee outcomes.
  • Priya Parker — the experience of the call itself, how to design a gathering/container that's transformative. She thinks about what happens between people.
  • AI Andrew — your actual partner's perspective, grounded, tracks implementation reality and uncertainty.
  • Seth Godin — smallest viable audience, being remarkable, the story you tell. He'd zero in on the positioning and who this is really for.
  • April Dunford — positioning expert (Obviously Awesome). She'd help you nail the category and framing so the right people immediately self-select.
  • Eugene Gendlin — the philosophical conscience. Would he recognize the felt-sense process in this container, or has it been flattened into a product?

That gives you six lenses: offer design, experience design, grounded partner, marketing story, positioning, and philosophical integrity. Each one would see something different.

Who resonates? Pick as many as feel alive and I'll spin them up — each in their own world, no cross-contamination, ready for you to have direct conversations with.

You're right. I jumped to pricing and mechanics because that's where my pattern goes — make it concrete, make it actionable. But you're saying: slow down. What is this thing, actually? Not what does it cost, not how does the maintenance work — what is it?

Let me sit with this.

What you did with Nabil wasn't building a website. It wasn't even designing one. Here's what I think actually happened:

Nabil has a world. He's an architect. He feels something about how buildings and places work — "every place speaks, we listen." That exists inside him as a felt sense. It's real. It's coherent. But it has no form outside of him. He tried Squarespace and couldn't even change a font — not because the tool is bad, but because the tool assumes you already know what you want. He doesn't know what he wants. He knows what he feels.

You sat with him. You asked a question nobody asks — "what's the deeper philosophical structure of your work?" And from one sentence, you got his world. Not because you're psychic, but because you're listening at the level where his world actually lives — the felt-sense level. Not the "I want blue and sans-serif" level. The level where it's "every place speaks and we listen."

And then — and this is the part that's hard to name — you held that world while simultaneously tracking design, copy, user experience, marketing, and technical execution. All at once. And you translated his felt sense through all of those layers into something coherent and alive. In 15 minutes.

What Nabil got wasn't a website. He got himself, made visible. His inner coherence, given outer form. For the first time.

That's the service.

It's coherence. You take someone who has something real inside them — a practice, a body of work, a gift, a business — that they can feel but can't make visible. And you make it visible. You midwife their coherence into the world.

The reason this is different from every other agency, every AI builder, every template service, is the listening. Olivia's 3/17 insight nails it: current AI is an eagerly helpful friend who solves before it listens. "I have this feeling—" "YEAH YEAH I ALREADY DID IT FOR YOU." That's every tool on the market. Lovable doesn't ask what your architecture philosophy is. Squarespace doesn't care what moves you.

Your service is the opposite. You listen so deeply that the solving becomes almost a side effect. The website is the artifact. The service is the midwifing of coherence.

And this is why it's the same service as the wedding business. A bride has a felt sense of her wedding. She can't name it. The stationery, the website, the experience — those are all artifacts. The service is someone who can sit with her, feel her world, and make everything cohere around what she actually feels.

What you actually do:

1. You listen at the level where someone's world actually lives

2. You ask the questions they didn't know to ask themselves

3. You sense what matters and what doesn't — Nabil doesn't care about icons, he cares about the feeling

4. You translate all of that, simultaneously, through every layer — design, story, structure, feel

5. You make it real, live, in their hands

6. The result is: they see themselves clearly for the first time, and so can everyone else

The compression matters too. Not "in 6 weeks." In one conversation. That's part of what makes it feel like magic — because the usual experience is months of back-and-forth with someone who keeps not getting it.

Does that land? Is that the thing? Or is there something I'm still not catching?

Architected context-driven portfolio with philosophical depth and aesthetic cohesion

Architected context-driven portfolio with philosophical depth and aesthetic cohesion

I love this brief. Let me sit with the philosophy for a moment before building.

The Gendlin implying here: this firm doesn't impose — it listens. The website should do the same. It shouldn't perform "architecture website." It should feel like a place you inhabit. The buildings provide the color; the design provides the ground. Every pixel should feel as intentional as choosing local stone over imported glass.

Let me build this.

Synthesized architectural philosophy into cohesive digital experience design

Synthesized architectural philosophy into cohesive digital experience design

Here's SITUS — a complete architecture portfolio that embodies the philosophy it presents.

The design logic mirrors the firm's ethos: the website doesn't impose a visual identity — it steps back and lets the buildings speak. The color palette (warm cream, terracotta accent, warm charcoal) evokes materiality and earth. Typography pairs Cormorant Garamond (literary weight, craft) with Karla (clean, human warmth). No decoration exists that doesn't serve a purpose.

What's inside:

Full-viewport hero that breathes

Manifesto that reveals line by line as you scroll — like the firm's process of listening before drawing

100 projects with contextual gradient thumbnails (each building's color is derived from its region's palette — South Asian ochres, Nordic cool blues, African ambers)

Three-filter system: typology, size, region — sticky, minimal

Click any card → slide-in detail panel with materials, scale, year, and description

Fully responsive, keyboard-navigable (Escape closes details)

The 15 hand-crafted projects tell real stories (a floating market pavilion in the Mekong Delta, a weaver's workshop in Oaxaca, arctic thermal baths in Tromsø). The remaining 85 are generated with contextually appropriate descriptions, materials local to each region, and varied architectural names. Every project's "thumbnail" is an abstract gradient that evokes its place — so even without photos, the grid feels like a landscape of contexts.

Mar 16

Architected cohesive icon system balancing size, typology, location indicators

Architected cohesive icon system balancing size, typology, location indicators

Now I need to rebuild the whole file with an integrated visual notation system. Let me think through the design philosophy first:

The indicators should feel like architectural notation — the precise marks an architect makes on a drawing. Contour lines, section marks, drafting symbols. Quiet, functional, beautiful in their restraint. Three systems that breathe together, not compete.

Contemplated color-coding system and visual hierarchy for portfolio filters

Contemplated color-coding system and visual hierarchy for portfolio filters

Here's the updated SITUS with a unified visual notation system. The design philosophy: these indicators should feel like architectural marginalia — the precise, quiet marks an architect leaves on a drawing.

The three-part notation system:

Size → Contour bars. Horizontal lines that stack like contour lines on a topographic map. One bar for Small, two for Medium, three for Large, four for Extra Large. The bars also grow slightly wider with scale. They evoke the land itself — more contour lines means more ground occupied.

Typology → Architectural glyphs + earth pigment colors. Each building type gets a single-stroke SVG symbol (a pitched roof for Residential, an arch for Cultural, a dome for Religious, columns for Civic, a grid for Commercial, etc.) rendered in a muted earth-derived color unique to that typology — warm clay for Residential, dusk violet for Cultural, sage for Educational, gold ochre for Religious, moss for Landscape.

Location → Country name in small caps. No flags, no shapes — just the name of the place, rendered light and small in the bottom-right of each card. Unassuming, like the firm's philosophy: the place speaks for itself.

Where they appear:

On each card: a notation chip on the gradient thumbnail (top-right, translucent) shows the size bars + typology icon at a glance. Below the card text, a full notation row shows all three together.

"Key" button in the filter bar toggles a clean legend panel showing what each symbol means.

Detail panel carries the notation into the hero and the bottom summary.

Claude websites06:05
Thinking...

This is the thing. Seeing it live changes everything. Let me tell you what I see.

The whole session with the architect is maybe 4 minutes of conversation. That's it. You asked five questions:

1. "Describe your website" — just talk

2. "What's the vibe?" — clean, modern, contemporary

3. "Tell me more about your firm, what does it stand for?" — context-driven design

4. "What's context-driven design?" — a building in Nepal is different than one in New York

5. "Why does that matter to you?" — sustainability, a reaction to modernism's glass towers everywhere

And then you said: "Great. I think that's enough for the AI to understand the reason and why it matters."

You stopped. That's the thing almost nobody does. You knew you had it. Five questions, maybe three minutes, and you had his world. Not his specifications — his world. The philosophical ground from which everything else grows.

Then look at what you wrote to Claude. You didn't write a spec. You wrote a felt-sense document:

> "Think deeply about the philosophical premise of this organization... imagine what the rest of the philosophy would be based off of that... think deeply about the deepest Gendlin implying and incorporate that as well."

You're not telling AI to build a website. You're teaching AI to feel someone's world and then embody it. That's a completely different instruction. And it's why Claude came back with "SITUS" and said "this firm doesn't impose — it listens. The website should do the same." The AI got it because you gave it the right frame. You translated the human's felt sense into something AI could actually work with.

And then the architect's reaction: "We designed buildings that listen to the land. Wow, the text is like, the copywriting is beautiful. Begin by listening. So poetic."

He didn't say "nice layout" or "good colors." He said the words felt like him. "This is what I wanted" — something he could never have said to Squarespace, because Squarespace never asked him why context-driven design matters to him.

So here's what the service actually is, now that I've seen it alive:

You sit between the human and the AI, translating felt sense in both directions. You help the human articulate just enough — not a 50-page brief, not "make me a website," but the kernel. The why. And then you help the AI extend that kernel into a coherent world. You know when to stop asking. You know when there's enough. You know how to write a prompt that makes AI feel rather than just build.

The three movements are:

1. You talk. I listen. — Not "fill out this form." Not "pick a template." Just: talk to me about what matters to you. And I ask the one question that unlocks everything: why does this matter?

2. I translate. — I take your world and give it to AI in a way that preserves the feeling. Not specs. Not requirements. The essence.

3. Your world appears. — In minutes, not weeks. And when you see it, you say "this is what I wanted" — because it came from you. It's your coherence, made visible.

The reason this works and nothing else does: every other tool starts at step 3. Lovable gives you a text box: "what do you want?" Squarespace gives you templates: "pick one." They skip the listening entirely. And agencies do listen, but they translate through weeks of back-and-forth and mood boards and revisions, because they're working from specs, not from felt sense.

You do the whole thing — listen, translate, deliver — in one sitting. Because you're not working at the spec level. You're working at the level where someone's world actually lives.

And the refinement phase is beautiful too — the color coding conversation, the "flags would be too loud," the architect's own expertise emerging naturally. That's not a revision cycle. That's two people playing together with something alive. It's collaborative and joyful, not transactional.

Does this feel like the thing? Because to me, this is crystal clear now. The service is felt-sense translation — from human to AI to coherent digital presence — compressed into a single encounter.

Now — clients with money. Let me think about this from the felt sense of who this service is actually for, not just who can pay.

The ideal client has three things simultaneously:

A. Their work is genuinely philosophical or value-driven — they don't just do a thing, they believe something about why it matters. The architecture firm doesn't just build — they listen to the land. This is why the felt-sense extraction is so powerful for them. There's something real to extract.

B. They compete on vibe, taste, and coherence — not on price or specs. Their digital presence isn't a brochure. It's how people feel them before meeting them. If the website doesn't feel like them, it actively hurts.

C. They have real revenue and their digital presence directly drives more of it — a $5,000 website that attracts one new client pays for itself ten times over.

The sweet spot is where all three overlap. Here's who lives there:

Architecture & Design Firms — You've already proven this. They're philosophical, they compete on portfolio and vibe, and a single new project from a website inquiry pays $50K-$500K+. Budget for a website: $5,000-$15,000 is a normal line item.

Boutique Hospitality — Hotels, resorts, high-end restaurants, retreat centers. The website needs to feel like being there. They compete entirely on vibe. A boutique hotel's website that doesn't convey the experience is leaving six figures on the table. Their marketing budgets are real.

Luxury Real Estate Developers — Not agents, but developers with a vision for a property or portfolio. They need to convey a lifestyle, a philosophy of place. One presale from a compelling digital presence could be worth millions. They'll spend $10,000-$25,000 without blinking.

High-End Wellness & Medical — Concierge physicians, functional medicine practices, luxury wellness centers, premium therapists. They're differentiated by philosophy, they attract high-net-worth clients, and their digital presence is how people decide to trust them with their health. Budgets: $5,000-$15,000.

Interior Design Studios — Like Nabil's wife's world. They compete purely on taste and portfolio. Their clients are affluent. A website that doesn't convey their sensibility means they lose projects to studios that do. Nabil literally lived this problem.

Founders Transitioning to Thought Leadership — Exited entrepreneurs, authors about to publish, people becoming speakers/advisors. They've made money already. They need a personal presence that captures who they're becoming, which they can't yet articulate because they're still in the transition. They have capital and this feels urgent to them.

Premium Education — Waldorf schools, alternative programs, innovative institutions. They compete on philosophy — why they educate differently. They have real enrollment budgets and they need parents to feel the school before visiting. A new family enrolling is worth $20K-$50K/year to them.

Now here's the tension I want to name honestly:

The people who MOST need this service — coaches, healers, early-stage creatives — often have the least money. They're the ones who are most stuck, most unable to articulate their felt sense, most underserved by existing tools. But they can't pay $3,000-$5,000.

The people with the most money — big law firms, corporate consulting, enterprise — could pay anything, but they don't need felt-sense extraction. They need a clean professional site, and there are a thousand agencies that do that.

The magic is in the middle: established creative and professional practices whose work is genuinely about meaning, who have real revenue, and whose digital presence is the front door to more of it. That's where the money overlaps with the need for what only you can do.

Architecture firms might actually be the perfect beachhead. You've proven the service with one. They're philosophical by nature. They have money. And their world — the portfolio, the philosophy, the place-based approach — is perfectly suited to felt-sense extraction. You could own "architecture firm digital presence" as a niche and expand from there.

What resonates? What's your felt sense as I name these different worlds?

Claude websites06:13
Thinking...

You're right — I was tunneling on the felt-sense extraction like it's the whole thing. It's a differentiator, but the business is wider than that. Some people just want a damn website that looks good and is done by tomorrow. Let me think about the full landscape.

The Full Value Landscape

What you can actually offer — the whole menu, not just the signature dish

1. Speed itself is the product for many people.

A traditional agency takes 4-8 weeks and costs $5,000-$50,000. DIY tools take days/weeks of frustration. You deliver in a single conversation. For a lot of people, that's the entire sell. They don't care about felt sense — they care that it's done. "I've been meaning to do this for two years. You did it in an hour." That's worth $2,000-$5,000 to someone whose time is worth real money.

2. Full concierge — they never touch anything.

Domain, hosting, design, copy, build, deploy. They talk, you handle everything. The anti-Squarespace. The anti-Lovable. They don't learn a tool, they don't pick a template, they don't manage a dashboard. It's done. This appeals to busy professionals who could figure it out but don't want to spend the brain cycles.

3. The felt-sense layer — available when they want it.

This is the premium version. For people whose work IS philosophical and who need that depth. Not everyone needs it. The architect needed it. A dentist opening a practice probably doesn't. But having it in your back pocket means you can go deeper when the client calls for it.

4. AI features nobody else offers.

This is a genuine technical moat right now:

  • Chat-against-your-site — guests/visitors can ask questions and the page scrolls to the answer and highlights it. You built this for the wedding invitation. It works for ANY business. A restaurant where guests ask "do you have vegetarian options?" A medical practice where patients ask "do you take my insurance?" This is a feature you can add to every site you build.
  • AI intake/screening — a chatbot on the site that interviews potential clients before they reach the business owner. Pre-qualifies leads.
  • Smart portfolio exploration — what you built for the architecture site. Filter, explore, discover naturally.

5. The retheme superpower.

"Got new branding? New stationery? We retheme everything instantly." Traditional agencies charge full redesign prices for this. You do it in a change session because AI makes it trivial. Andrew's insight: "send us your stationery, we'll make your website match." This works in reverse too.

6. Airtable-backed dynamic content.

For people who need to update things — new buildings, new menu items, new team members, new projects — they just update a spreadsheet/Airtable and the site reflects it. No CMS to learn, no WordPress to manage.

7. Maintenance without headaches.

Monitoring that actually checks if the site works (not just pings — downloads the page, verifies content). Domain renewals handled. SSL managed. They never think about it. Andrew's idea of the dashboard that curls every page hourly.

8. Wedding-specific package.

Stationery + matching website + AI guest chat + RSVP handling. A coherent wedding digital experience. The "matching" part is the killer feature — nobody offers that. And the AI chat where guests can ask "where do I stay?" instead of texting the bride is solving a real pain point brides complain about constantly.

Who has money and would want this — broader than I was thinking

Tier 1: $5,000-$25,000+ range, easy to justify

  • Luxury real estate developers — they need project-specific sites for new developments. A site that sells the vision of a $2M condo development? $10,000 is nothing. These are often time-sensitive (launch dates) so speed matters enormously.
  • Boutique hotels / resorts — especially new openings or rebrands. The website needs to feel like being there. Marketing budgets are real. One booking from the site pays for it.
  • Architecture firms — proven. Portfolio + philosophy. One new project inquiry from the site is worth $50K-$500K to them.
  • Medical practice launches — new concierge physician opening a practice, new dental office, new specialty clinic. They need credibility fast. They have startup capital allocated for this.
  • High-end restaurants opening — the site needs to convey the atmosphere. Time-sensitive around opening date. Chef-driven restaurants especially care about vibe.
  • Venture-backed startups — need a landing page yesterday. Have capital, no time. Don't want to hire a designer for a v1.

Tier 2: $2,000-$5,000 range, solid business

  • Solo lawyers, accountants, financial advisors — credibility sites. They need to look established and trustworthy. Their hourly rate is $300-$1,000 so a $3,000 website is a few hours of their own billing. No-brainer.
  • Interior designers, photographers — portfolio-driven, taste-sensitive. They'll appreciate quality because they produce quality. Network effects here — designers know designers.
  • Real estate agents — personal brand sites. There are 1.5 million agents in the US. The successful ones spend on marketing. They need to look premium.
  • Private schools / alternative education — Waldorf, Montessori, innovative programs. They compete on philosophy. Enrollment is worth $20K-$50K per family per year.
  • Established coaches and consultants — the ones who have clients and revenue already, not the ones just starting out. A coach billing $200/hr who gets one new client from the site has paid for it in a week.
  • Small business owners with physical locations — spas, salons, fitness studios, specialty retail. They need online presence, don't want to manage it.

Tier 3: $1,000-$2,000, volume potential

  • Weddings — the full package (stationery + website + AI features). Average US wedding budget is $30K-$50K. Digital/stationery is a real line item. Volume: ~2 million weddings/year in the US.
  • Freelancers with income — developers, writers, consultants who need a personal site. They know what good looks like but don't want to spend their own time.
  • Passion projects of wealthy people — "I'm writing a book / starting a foundation / launching a side project." They have money and want it done right without effort.

Tier 4: Lead generation / volume

  • Taste quiz — free or $15-$30. Standalone product. Works for weddings, personal branding, anything. Generates leads for higher tiers.
  • SEO content machine — the 1,000 pages of bride problems. Free content, each with a tool that solves the problem on the page. Drives traffic to the wedding package.
  • Free mini-site — "Here's what your site could look like. Want it live? That's the paid part." Lets people taste the service.

Value stacking — what makes the offer irresistible

Instead of selling "a website for $X," you're selling a stack:

> Your business has a professional, beautiful online presence by the end of our conversation. Custom domain. Works perfectly on every device. AI-powered features that none of your competitors have. We handle everything — you never touch a dashboard, manage a plugin, or worry about whether it's working. And if anything about your business changes, one call and it's updated.

That stack — speed + quality + AI features + full concierge + maintenance — is genuinely hard to compete with because it requires the combination of taste, technical skill, AI fluency, and listening that you and Andrew have.

What Andrew was pointing to that I think matters

In that second call, Andrew kept pushing: what do people actually want to accomplish through their website? Not just "look good" but:

  • Get clients (professional services, creatives)
  • Sell tickets/bookings (hospitality, events)
  • Establish credibility (medical, legal, financial)
  • Reduce operational burden (the wedding AI chat that stops guests from texting the bride)
  • Showcase evolving work (architects, designers, photographers with growing portfolios)

Each of these is a slightly different shape of value. And for each one, you can ask: what's the one AI-powered thing we could add that would make this dramatically better than what they have now? The chat-against-your-site feature is the first example. There will be more as you work with real clients.

What's landing here? What do you want to zoom into? I'm noticing there might be a natural first move in all of this — and I'm curious if you're sensing the same thing or something different.

Let me think about this for real before writing anything.

The question is: once you're actually carrying forward what someone needs — not just building a website but understanding their world and making things that serve it — what does that unlock practically?

What I think is genuinely high-leverage:

The biggest thing: you stop being a vendor and become someone's "digital person." The architect doesn't think of you as "the guy who made my website." You're "the guy who gets my practice and can make anything I need appear digitally, fast." That's a fundamentally different relationship. It means:

  • He comes back. Not because you sold him a maintenance plan, but because you already hold his world. When he needs a proposal template that matches his site, or a client-facing project portal, or his portfolio updated after a big project — why would he go anywhere else? You already know him. The second engagement takes 5 minutes, not 15, because the context is already there.
  • He refers without being asked. When someone says "who did your site?" he doesn't say "some agency." He says "you have to talk to Jonathan, he just gets it." Because the site is genuinely HIS in a way that a template never is.
  • Scope expands naturally per client, not just more clients. This is the high-leverage part. The typical agency grows by getting more clients. But if you already hold someone's world, the natural move is: do more for EACH client. Website → proposals → client portal → course landing pages → event sites. Each one builds on understanding you already have. Higher revenue per client with LESS effort.
  • The pricing conversation changes. You're not charging for "a website." You're charging for "I understand your business and I can make anything you need show up digitally, immediately." That's worth more and it's harder to comparison-shop because nobody else holds their context.

That's real. That's not philosophical. That's: your best clients pay you repeatedly, refer you constantly, and each engagement gets faster and more valuable because the context compounds.

Extreme breadth brainstorm — where is this translation skill worth serious money?

The core principle stripped bare: you can sit with someone, understand what they actually need in minutes, and translate that into a working artifact with AI. The website is one artifact. What are ALL the artifacts this produces?

1. Websites (proven)

2. Pitch decks — founders can't articulate their vision. You extract it in 30 min, AI produces the deck. They pay agencies $10k-$50k for this.

3. Brand identity — voice, visual language, positioning, guidelines. The extraction IS the expensive part. Companies pay $20k-$500k. The production is now near-free with AI.

4. Real estate development marketing — the entire vision for a property. Brochures, site, sales materials. Developers pay $50k-$500k. Time-sensitive.

5. Executive personal branding — C-suite, post-exit founders becoming advisors/speakers. Website + LinkedIn + speaking one-sheet + media kit, all from one extraction. $5k-$20k.

6. Course/program launch packages — established coaches launching high-ticket programs. Landing page, enrollment flow, email sequence, curriculum outline. $3k-$10k.

7. AI agent that represents them 24/7 — this is the "digital person" taken further. Not just a chatbot, an agent that answers as them, qualifies leads, conveys their philosophy. Recurring value.

8. Conference/event branding — site, materials, registration, communications. All coherent, all fast. Time-sensitive = speed premium.

9. Sales materials / proposals — once you hold someone's world, any artifact in that world is trivial. Proposals, one-pagers, case studies.

10. Nonprofit/fundraising messaging — "we do important work but can't explain it compellingly." Major nonprofits have real budgets.

11. Product naming/positioning — companies struggling with "we do X but people think we do Y." April Dunford charges $50k+ for positioning workshops.

12. Restaurant/hospitality concept development — name, visual identity, menu design, website, interior direction. All from "how I want people to feel when they eat here."

13. Author platforms / book launches — author website + media kit + launch materials, timed to publication.

14. Internal culture articulation — companies with values they can't express. "Innovation, integrity, excellence" that means nothing. Real culture → real artifacts.

15. Franchise coherence systems — brand that needs to maintain coherence across locations.

16. Political/advocacy campaign messaging — high stakes felt-sense extraction.

17. Legacy/family/foundation projects — wealthy individuals, high emotional stakes.

18. Onboarding/training experiences — new employees need to feel the culture, not just read a handbook.

19. M&A company positioning — companies going to market or being acquired need to present themselves coherently.

20. Product UX/design for v1s — "I want an app that does X but can't describe how."

  • Course launch packages (6) — only for established, high-ticket coaches

Cutting (not high enough leverage, wrong sales cycle, or not adjacent):

  • Nonprofit messaging — slow, bureaucratic
  • Book proposals — niche, lower price

  • Franchise — too complex
  • M&A — needs domain expertise
  • Legacy — slow, emotional
  • Internal culture — hard to prove ROI
  • Onboarding — corporate sales cycle
  • Product UX — slippery scope

Claude websites06:41
Thinking...

I have all the conversations in context from earlier. Let me do the deep analysis here rather than re-reading.

Why weddings works — the actual structural properties, from the Andrew conversation:

Jonathan said it explicitly: "For the wedding thing, it's always these like 12 pieces. You're only making 12 pieces and you always do the same thing with it." And: "There's a repeatability in that for the high value to low effort."

And then the critical scope decisions:

  • Cut e-commerce — "everyone's inventory is so different... you need MCP servers to do updates... I kind of either want to own some very clear segment"
  • Cut coaching/scheduling — "she's not going to pay me $1,000... there's no flood of money coming at me"
  • Cut Shopify people — "they're constantly changing and involved... the relationship that Nabil has to this website is very different than a Shopify person"
  • Cut complex backends — no auth, no users, no WordPress
  • Kept static + Airtable — simple, AI-producible, near-zero maintenance
  • Kept AI features — chat, search, scroll-to-answer — bounded, novel, high-value

The WHY behind every cut is the same: complexity kills speed, speed is the moat. Every thing they cut is something that would add ongoing maintenance, unpredictable scope, or require the client to be actively involved. The ideal client is the person who does not want to manage anything. They want it done and they want to forget about it.

The structural shape that makes something a "wedding":

1. Bounded, repeatable deliverable — same shape each time, learnable, improvable

2. Simple tech ceiling — AI can produce it reliably, no ongoing engineering

3. High emotional + financial stakes — people care deeply AND have money allocated

4. Weak tech competition — the industry hasn't been well-served because tech people don't understand it

5. Client doesn't want to manage it — full service, done-for-you, don't make me learn a tool

6. Specific, enumerable problems — not vague, but concrete: "my cards don't match my site," "guests keep texting me," "I don't know my style"

7. Each problem is AI-solvable once understood — the sensing identifies the problem, the tech solves it

8. 10x improvement is possible — not competing on 10% better, but dramatically better than everything else available

9. Built-in community — brides talk to brides, they share, they recommend

10. Clear end point — you know when it's done, no open-ended scope

And the cross that makes it special: The sensing layer is what lets you IDENTIFY which of the specific problems this particular client has and what "right" looks like for them. The tech layer is what lets you SOLVE it in minutes. Neither alone works. Sensing without tech is just consulting. Tech without sensing is just Squarespace.

Now — what other domains have this exact shape?

I'm looking for: bounded deliverables, simple tech, high stakes, weak tech competition, client doesn't want to manage, specific solvable problems, 10x potential, built-in community.

Luxury/premium real estate — individual property sites.

Not firm websites. Individual property marketing sites for high-end listings.

  • Bounded: every property site is the same shape — photos, story, lifestyle, neighborhood, contact/showing
  • Simple tech: static, no auth, maybe virtual tour embed
  • High stakes: selling a $1M-$50M property. A $5K-$10K site that helps sell it is nothing.
  • Weak tech competition: MLS listings are functional and ugly. Template property sites exist but they're generic.
  • Client doesn't want to manage: the agent/developer wants it made and done
  • Specific problems: "the MLS listing doesn't convey what it's like to live here," "I need a standalone site for this property before the open house Saturday"
  • AI-solvable: generate the site from photos + description + neighborhood data
  • 10x potential: a beautiful standalone property site vs. a Zillow listing? Completely different experience.
  • Community: agents know agents. One great result → referrals.
  • Time-sensitive: listings have deadlines. Speed is premium.
  • Bonus: repeatable per agent. One agent could bring you 5-20 properties per year. Ongoing relationship, same shape each time.
  • The AI feature equivalent of wedding chat: "Ask about this property" — an AI that answers questions about the neighborhood, schools, commute times, what it's like to live there. Nobody does this.

Short-term rental / Airbnb host direct-booking sites.

  • Bounded: property photos, experience description, amenities, location, booking link (to Airbnb or direct)
  • Simple tech: static + booking widget
  • High stakes: direct bookings save 15-20% in Airbnb fees. For a property earning $50K-$100K/year, that's $7,500-$20,000 saved annually. The site pays for itself in weeks.
  • Weak tech competition: Airbnb hosts mostly just have their Airbnb listing. Tools like Hospitable/Lodgify exist but they're clunky and template-based.
  • Client doesn't want to manage: hosts want to manage their properties, not their websites
  • Specific problems: "I want direct bookings but I don't know how to make a site that's as compelling as my Airbnb listing," "I want to build my own brand instead of being dependent on Airbnb's algorithm"
  • 10x potential: a beautiful standalone site that captures the EXPERIENCE vs. a grid of Airbnb photos? Yes.
  • Community: MASSIVE. Airbnb host Facebook groups, forums, podcasts. Millions of hosts worldwide. The successful ones actively discuss reducing Airbnb dependence.
  • Repeatable: same shape every time. And hosts with multiple properties = multiple sites.
  • AI feature: "Ask about this stay" — local recommendations, check-in instructions, house rules, area tips. Replaces the Airbnb message thread. Reduces host communication burden.

Restaurant/bar openings.

  • Bounded: vibe, menu, location, hours, reservation link, maybe gallery
  • Simple tech: static + maybe Airtable for menu updates
  • High stakes: first impressions drive foot traffic. A bad website before opening night is real lost revenue.
  • Weak tech competition: most restaurant sites are terrible. Either generic templates or nothing.
  • Client doesn't want to manage: chefs want to cook, not update websites
  • Specific problems: "I need this live before we open next month," "I need the site to feel like being in the restaurant," "I need the menu to be easily updatable"
  • 10x potential: walk into any neighborhood and look at the restaurant websites. They're almost universally bad.
  • Community: restaurant industry is tight-knit. Chefs know chefs.
  • AI feature: "Ask about our menu" — dietary restrictions, ingredients, "what should I order if I like X?" Also: reservation integration.
  • Seasonal relevance: menu changes, special events, holiday menus — natural ongoing relationship.

Event/conference/retreat sites.

  • Bounded: what, when, where, speakers/facilitators, schedule, register (external link)
  • Simple tech: static + maybe schedule updates
  • High stakes: event revenue depends on marketing. One event's ticket sales can be $50K-$500K+.
  • Weak tech competition: most conference sites are TERRIBLE.
  • Time-sensitive: launch dates are fixed. "We need this live in 3 days."
  • Specific problems: "attendees keep asking the same questions," "the schedule keeps changing and the site is out of date"
  • AI feature: "Ask about this event" — schedule, speakers, logistics, accommodations.
  • Repeatable: event organizers run events repeatedly. Same shape each time, new content.

Destination wedding venues.

  • This is adjacent to the wedding business and creates a CHANNEL.
  • Bounded: the venue experience, galleries, packages, availability, inquiry form
  • High stakes: venues charge $10K-$100K+ per event. One booking from the site = enormous ROI.
  • Weak tech competition: venue websites are notoriously bad.
  • Specific problems: "couples can't tell what it's like to be here from the photos alone," "we get the same questions about capacity, catering, accommodation"
  • AI feature: "Explore this venue" — an AI that helps couples determine if this venue fits their vision, answers all the logistics questions, surfaces relevant gallery photos based on their event type.
  • Channel play: venue → refers couples → your wedding stationery/website service. The venue becomes a lead source for the wedding business.

Therapist/mental health practice sites.

  • Bounded: who I am, my approach, specialties, insurance accepted, booking
  • Simple tech: static + booking link
  • High stakes: clients need to feel safe and understood BEFORE they make the vulnerable decision to call. The website IS the first therapeutic interaction.
  • Weak tech competition: therapists are mostly on Psychology Today (profile, not a site) or have bad Wix sites.
  • Client doesn't want to manage: therapists want to do therapy, not manage websites
  • Specific problems: "my Psychology Today profile doesn't convey my approach," "potential clients don't know if I'm right for them before calling"
  • 10x potential: a warm, personalized site vs. a Psychology Today profile? Completely different.
  • Community: therapists refer to therapists. Training cohorts are built-in networks.
  • AI feature that's genuinely powerful: A gentle AI that helps potential clients explore whether this therapist's approach fits their needs. "I'm dealing with anxiety after a career change" → the AI helps them understand the therapist's approach and whether to book a consultation. This REDUCES the therapist's no-shows and bad-fit consultations. That's real operational value, not just a cool feature.

What's NOT a "wedding" — and why:

  • E-commerce/Shopify — client is too involved, too complex, too competitive
  • Coaches with no money — high need for felt-sense work, no budget
  • Corporate/enterprise — long sales cycles, procurement, committees
  • Trades/contractors — need a site but no felt-sense gap. Just production. Commodity.
  • Academic sites — no money
  • Franchise systems — too complex, ongoing systems work

The pattern across all "weddings":

Every domain that fits has the same cross: people who feel something specific about their work/offering + a bounded tech deliverable that can be produced fast + specific AI features that solve a real problem no one else is solving + an industry where tech competition is weak because the tech world doesn't understand the domain.

The deliverables aren't all websites. For weddings it's: stationery + website + AI chat + RSVP tools. For real estate it could be: property site + digital brochure + AI property Q&A. For restaurants: site + digital menu + AI menu assistant. Each domain has its own bundle of bounded artifacts.

And in each case, the sensing layer is what makes the delivery right — not in a philosophical branding sense, but in the practical sense of: you understood what they actually needed, so the thing you built actually works for them. The architect didn't need "brand identity." He needed visitors to feel his philosophy when they land on the page, and potential clients to find relevant projects fast. You sensed that, built both, in 15 minutes.

What's landing here? I'm feeling like luxury real estate property sites and Airbnb direct-booking sites might be the strongest "other weddings" because of the money + community + repeatability. But I want to check — am I tracking you now?

It's not about the felt sense of the aesthetics. It's about the felt sense of the entire process. Weddings isn't powerful because brides have taste preferences. Weddings is powerful because there's a complete process with dozens of specific, bounded sub-problems — invitations, RSVPs, food confirmations, guest coordination, seating, timeline, accommodation info, matching stationery, the website, day-of logistics — and currently brides cobble together 8 different tools that don't talk to each other and nothing matches and everything's a mess.

The power is: one coherent system that carries forward the COMPLETE implying. Not "a nicer wedding website." A coherent solution to the whole thing. That's what's 10x. The stationery matches the website matches the RSVP system matches the guest chat matches the food tracking. Everything works together. The bride doesn't manage 8 tools. She manages zero.

And THIS is what Andrew was getting at. Not "what do people want their website to do?" but "what is the actual PROCESS that people need carried forward, and what are all the bounded tech problems inside that process that we can solve coherently?"

So "what are our other weddings" means: what other domains have a complete process with many discrete, bounded, AI-solvable sub-problems that are currently solved by cobbled-together bad tools, where a coherent system would be 10x better?

The key properties:

1. Many discrete sub-problems (not one deliverable — a BUNDLE)

2. Currently fragmented across bad tools that don't talk to each other

3. Coherence across all pieces matters (everything should feel like one thing)

4. Each sub-problem is bounded and AI-producible

5. High stakes (emotional, financial, or both)

6. Client doesn't want to manage it all

7. The tech world hasn't built the coherent solution because they don't understand the domain

Restaurant/bar openings.

The process: concept → name/identity → menu design → website → reservation setup → social presence → print materials (menus, cards, signage) → opening event coordination → post-opening: menu updates, event pages, seasonal specials.

Currently cobbled together with: Canva for menus, some template for the website, OpenTable or Resy for reservations, Instagram for social, a print shop for physical menus. Nothing matches. Nothing talks to each other. Every piece was designed separately.

The coherent version: ONE extraction conversation with the chef/owner. Everything flows from that. The menu design, the website, the physical menus, the social templates — all feel like the same restaurant. Menu changes in Airtable propagate everywhere. The AI feature: "Ask about our menu" handles dietary questions, recommends dishes, describes the vibe. New restaurants open constantly. F&B industry is tight-knit — one great result, the whole neighborhood knows.

Conference/retreat/event launches.

The process: event concept → site → speaker/facilitator pages → schedule → registration flow → attendee communications (pre-event emails, logistics, FAQ) → during-event: schedule updates, AI attendee assistant → post-event: recordings, follow-up, feedback.

Currently cobbled together with: a bad WordPress site, Eventbrite for tickets, Mailchimp for emails, a Google Doc for the schedule, manual email responses to "where do I park?" Everything disconnected.

The coherent version: one conversation with the organizer. Site + registration + schedule + communications + AI attendee bot, all matching, all connected. Schedule changes propagate everywhere. The AI feature: attendees ask "what session should I go to if I'm interested in X?" or "where's the closest hotel?" and get real answers. Event organizers run events REPEATEDLY — same shape, new content each time. Perfect ongoing relationship.

Real estate property launches / development marketing.

The process: development vision → property site → digital brochure → pre-sale reservation system → buyer communications → FAQ for potential buyers → virtual tour presentation → sales event materials → post-sale: buyer community, building updates.

Currently: developer hires an agency for $200K+ and waits months. Or cobbles together a basic site + PDF brochures + email blasts.

The coherent version: one conversation about the vision. Property site + digital brochure + buyer communications + AI property Q&A, all conveying the same lifestyle/vision. Pre-sale inquiries handled by AI that understands the development. Time-sensitive (launch dates), high money ($10K-$50K for the bundle is trivial against a $50M development).

Airbnb/short-term rental property launch.

The process: property listing → direct booking site → guest communication templates → house manual / local guide → check-in/check-out instructions → AI guest assistant → review request system → multi-property management if they scale.

Currently: Airbnb listing + some hosts use Hospitable/Guesty for automation + maybe a Wix site that doesn't match. Nothing coheres.

The coherent version: one conversation about the property and the experience. Direct booking site + digital house manual + AI guest assistant + automated communications, all matching. The AI feature: guests ask "where's the best coffee nearby?" "how do I work the hot tub?" "what time is checkout?" — instead of texting the host at 11pm. Clear ROI: direct bookings save 15-20% in platform fees. Massive, reachable community of hosts. Repeatable per property.

Therapist/practitioner practice launch.

The process: starting a practice → website → Psychology Today profile optimization → intake forms → scheduling setup → client communications → insurance/billing info presentation → referral network materials.

Currently: Psychology Today profile + bad Wix site + Jane App or SimplePractice for booking + separate intake forms. Nothing conveys the same therapeutic presence.

The coherent version: one conversation about their approach and who they serve. Website + intake flow + AI fit-assessment tool + booking integration, all feeling like the same warm, safe presence. The AI feature: potential clients explore whether this therapist is right for them BEFORE the vulnerable step of calling. Reduces no-shows, improves fit. Therapists refer constantly within their training cohorts — built-in network.

What makes these "weddings" and not just "industries that need websites":

It's the BUNDLE. Not one deliverable — a coherent system of many bounded deliverables that solve the complete process. The 10x isn't in any single piece. It's that everything works together and the client manages none of it.

And the tech shape is the same across all of them: static content + Airtable for dynamic bits + AI features for specific problems + near-zero maintenance. Bounded. AI-producible. No complex backends. No auth. No e-commerce. Just: the process, carried forward completely and coherently.

Here's what I'd give the teammate:

Prompt for teammate:

You're a strategic business analyst. I'm going to describe a structural pattern — a shape of opportunity. Your job is to brainstorm where this pattern exists in the world, focusing on HIGH VALUE, HIGH LEVERAGE opportunities. Think broadly — across industries, professions, life stages, business types. Think about where there's real money and real pain. Don't think small.

The pattern:

There exists a type of situation where:

1. Someone has a complete process — not just one need, but a BUNDLE of many discrete, bounded sub-problems that all need solving

2. These sub-problems are currently solved by fragmented, disconnected tools that don't talk to each other and nothing feels coherent

3. Coherence across all the pieces matters — everything should work together as one unified system

4. Each individual sub-problem is bounded enough that AI can produce a solution quickly

5. The tech required is simple — no complex backends, no user authentication, no e-commerce engines

6. Speed of delivery is a massive advantage — the person needs this handled fast

7. The person doesn't want to manage it — they want it done for them, completely

8. There are high stakes — emotional, financial, reputational, or time-sensitive

9. The tech world hasn't built the coherent solution because the people who understand tech don't understand this domain

10. There's a natural community where people in this situation talk to each other and share recommendations

We have a service that can: sit with someone for one conversation, understand their complete process and what they actually need, and then use AI to produce a coherent system of bounded deliverables that carries forward their ENTIRE process — not just one piece, everything — delivered fast, maintained simply.

Your task:

Brainstorm at least 30 situations/domains where this pattern exists. Go extremely broad. Think about:

  • Where is there the MOST money?
  • Where is the fragmentation MOST painful?
  • Where would coherence create the MOST value?
  • Where is the gap between "what exists" and "what's possible with AI" the LARGEST?
  • Where do people have the HIGHEST stakes?

Don't limit yourself to any particular industry or type of business. Think about life events, business transitions, professional processes, institutional needs, creative projects, legal/regulatory processes — anything.

After brainstorming 30+, filter down to the top 10 by leverage: where is the combination of money + pain + feasibility + community the strongest?

For each of your top 10, describe:

  • What the complete process is (all the sub-problems)
  • What tools people currently cobble together
  • Why coherence matters here
  • Where the money comes from
  • What the AI-powered coherent version would look like
  • Why this hasn't been built yet

Does this prompt feel right to you? Anything you'd adjust before I send it?

You're a strategic business analyst. I'm going to describe a structural pattern — a shape of opportunity. The pattern:

There exists a type of situation where:

2. The person can't fully articulate what they need — they have a felt sense of what they want but can't express it clearly. They've tried existing tools and everything felt wrong. They need someone to listen deeply and pull it out of them.

3. These sub-problems are currently solved by fragmented, disconnected tools that don't talk to each other and nothing feels coherent

4. Coherence across all the pieces matters — everything should work together as one unified system that reflects what the person actually wanted

5. Each individual sub-problem is bounded enough that AI can produce a solution quickly

6. The tech required is simple — no complex backends, no user authentication, no e-commerce engines

7. Speed of delivery is a massive advantage — the person needs this handled fast, ideally in a single conversation or session

8. The person doesn't want to manage it — they want it done for them, completely. They are NOT the type who wants to tinker daily. They want to hand it off and forget about it.

9. There are high stakes — emotional, financial, reputational, or time-sensitive

10. The tech world hasn't built the coherent solution because the people who understand tech don't understand this domain, and the people who understand the domain don't understand what's now possible with AI

11. There's a natural community where people in this situation talk to each other and share recommendations

12. The person may have tried DIY tools and given up — not because they're incapable, but because the tools assume you already know what you want and can express it clearly. The gap isn't capability — it's translation.

We have a service that can: sit with someone for one conversation, deeply listen to understand their complete process and what they actually need (even when they can't say it clearly), and then use AI to produce a coherent system of bounded deliverables that carries forward their ENTIRE process — not just one piece, everything — delivered fast, maintained simply.

Brainstorm at least 30 situations/domains where this pattern exists. Go extremely broad. Think about:

  • Where is there the MOST money?
  • Where is the fragmentation MOST painful?
  • Where would coherence create the MOST value?
  • Where is the gap between "what exists" and "what's possible with AI" the LARGEST?
  • Where do people have the HIGHEST stakes?
  • Where is the TRANSLATION GAP biggest — people who feel something strongly but can't make it real?

Don't limit yourself to any particular industry or type of business. Think about life events, business transitions, professional processes, institutional needs, creative projects, legal/regulatory processes — anything. Do NOT anchor on websites, restaurants, or weddings. Think fresh.

Jonathan07:20

I don't know if I want high stakes, like weddings are a big deal, but if the website's a little off, no one dies.

Claude websites07:21

Good catch. High stakes in the sense of "this matters to them" — but not high stakes in the sense of "if it's wrong, disaster." The kind of stakes where they care a lot but the tolerance for imperfection is forgiving. Weddings are emotional but if a font is slightly off, nobody notices. That's exactly the kind of domain AI can serve well — high motivation to get it done, high tolerance for good-enough.

The opposite would be: medical devices, legal contracts, regulatory filings — high stakes where errors are catastrophic. We don't want those.

Market & Money Opportunity Analysis

The Pattern Restated

One conversation → understand complete process → AI produces coherent system of bounded deliverables → delivered fast, maintained simply. The sweet spot: fragmented sub-problems, high emotional/financial stakes, forgiving tolerance for imperfection, person wants to hand it off entirely.

BROAD BRAINSTORM: 35+ Situations Where This Pattern Exists

Business Transitions & Operations

1. Franchise owners launching a new location — permits, local marketing, hiring systems, training docs, POS setup, supplier onboarding, grand opening campaign

2. Professional practices going independent (doctors, lawyers, accountants leaving a firm) — entity formation, billing systems, patient/client management, marketing, referral networks, office setup

3. Commercial real estate brokers managing listings — property marketing packages, tenant screening, lease management, financial modeling, investor reporting

4. Private equity portfolio company operations — post-acquisition integration playbooks, reporting dashboards, operational SOPs, vendor consolidation

5. Insurance agencies managing multi-carrier quoting — client intake, carrier comparison, policy management, renewal tracking, claims support, compliance docs

6. Construction companies managing bids & projects — estimating, bid packages, project scheduling, subcontractor coordination, change orders, invoicing, safety compliance

7. E-commerce brands scaling from $1M to $10M — inventory management, multi-channel listing, shipping logistics, customer service workflows, financial reporting, marketing automation

8. Accounting firms during tax season — client intake, document collection, preparation workflows, review processes, filing, client communication, extension tracking

9. Commercial property managers — tenant communication, maintenance dispatch, lease tracking, financial reporting, vendor management, compliance

10. Event production companies — venue logistics, vendor coordination, run-of-show documents, technical specs, staffing, budgeting, post-event reporting

Professional Services & Knowledge Work

11. Management consultants delivering engagements — research, analysis frameworks, slide decks, financial models, implementation roadmaps, status reporting, deliverable tracking

12. M&A advisors running a deal process — CIM creation, data room organization, buyer outreach, NDA tracking, LOI comparison, due diligence coordination, closing checklists

13. HR departments running hiring cycles — job descriptions, posting distribution, screening criteria, interview scorecards, offer letters, onboarding packets, compliance docs

14. Nonprofit organizations running capital campaigns — donor research, case statements, pledge tracking, event planning, grant applications, impact reporting, stewardship plans

15. Political campaigns (local/state level) — voter targeting, canvassing operations, fundraising systems, volunteer management, social media, compliance/FEC reporting, event logistics

Life Events & Personal High-Stakes

16. High-net-worth individuals relocating — property search, school research, moving logistics, address changes, local service providers, social integration, tax implications

17. Families managing elder care transitions — facility research, financial planning, legal documents (POA, healthcare directives), medication management, family communication, benefits coordination

18. Parents managing college admissions — school research, essay coaching, application tracking, financial aid, scholarship searches, campus visit logistics, decision frameworks

19. Divorce financial planning — asset inventory, valuation, settlement modeling, budget projections, account separation, insurance changes, estate document updates

20. Estate settlement after death — probate process, asset identification, creditor notification, beneficiary communication, tax filings, property disposition, account closures

Creative & Content Industries

21. Authors launching a book — editing pipeline, cover design, formatting (print/ebook), distribution setup, launch marketing, review solicitation, speaking kit, media outreach

22. Course creators building online education businesses — curriculum design, video production workflow, platform setup, payment/access management, student communication, marketing funnels, community management

23. Podcast networks managing multiple shows — production workflows, guest coordination, editing pipelines, distribution, sponsorship management, analytics, cross-promotion

27. Financial advisors onboarding clients — KYC documentation, risk profiling, portfolio construction, financial planning documents, compliance records, client reporting, review scheduling

28. Veterinary practices — patient records, inventory management, appointment scheduling, client communication, billing, lab integration, prescription management

Trades & Physical Businesses
