Claude websites08:16
Thinking...

You're right, and I hear the filter now more clearly. Let me apply the actual "wedding test":

  • All the same SHAPE — same sub-problems every time (repeatable)
  • Different in what they CARE about — personalization/taste, not structural uniqueness
  • End users actually USE the outputs — like guests use invitations, RSVP, the website
  • Concentrated community — like brides on Reddit, not scattered
  • Real money — budget exists for this
  • Forgiving tolerance — nobody gets hurt if something's slightly off
  • Bounded timeline — there's a deadline or trigger

Let me write each one the same way and sort by what I think actually works.

Tier 1 — Most "wedding-like"

Vacation Rental / Airbnb Property Setup

What it is: Setting up a short-term rental property for guests — everything from listing to guest experience to direct bookings.

The "12 pieces": Property listing copy, direct booking site, guest welcome communications, house manual, local area guide, check-in/check-out instructions, AI guest FAQ ("how does the hot tub work?", "best coffee nearby?"), review request sequence, pricing guidance, cleaning/turnover checklist, emergency info sheet, repeat guest outreach.

Who uses the outputs: GUESTS use almost everything — the listing, the site, the manual, the guide, the chat. Just like wedding guests use the invitation and website.

Community: Massive. Airbnb host Facebook groups (100K+ members each), BiggerPockets (2.5M members), STR podcasts and forums. Hosts share EVERYTHING that works.

Money: Properties earn $30K-$150K/year. Direct bookings save 15-20% in Airbnb fees ($5K-$30K/year saved). A $2-5K setup pays for itself in months. Hosts with multiple properties = repeat client.

Why it fits: Structurally the most similar to weddings. Every property needs the same pieces. The personalization is in the character of the property and the experience, not in the structure. Guests are the end users. Massive concentrated community. Clear ROI. Speed matters (the sooner you're live, the sooner you earn). Host doesn't want to manage the tech — they want to manage the property.

Real Estate Agent Personal System

What it is: The complete client-facing system an agent needs to win listings, serve buyers/sellers, and generate referrals.

The "12 pieces": Personal brand website, listing presentation template, buyer/seller guides, property marketing templates (flyer, social posts, email blast), CMA presentation, client communication sequences (new lead → closing → anniversary), open house system, review generation workflow, referral request system, neighborhood guides, "just listed" / "just sold" packages.

Who uses the outputs: CLIENTS use everything — the website, the guides, the property marketing, the communications. Other agents see the listing materials.

Community: Very tight. Brokerages have training programs, NAR has 1.5M members, there are massive Facebook groups, coaching programs (Tom Ferry, etc.), and agents talk to agents constantly.

Money: Agents in the top 20% earn $100K+. One additional closed deal from better materials = $5-15K in commission. They already spend $2-5K/year on marketing tools that don't cohere. They'd pay $3-8K for a complete system.

Why it fits: Every agent needs the same pieces. The personalization is in their market, personality, and niche (luxury vs. first-time buyers) — not in the structure. Clients actually use and see everything. Huge concentrated community. Clear ROI. High tolerance for imperfection.

Financial Advisor Client System

What it is: The complete client experience system — from prospect to long-term client — that makes an advisor look established and trustworthy.

The "12 pieces": Client onboarding packet, risk assessment questionnaire, investment policy statement template, financial plan presentation format, portfolio review template, quarterly client communication, annual review meeting framework, referral request system, prospect nurture sequence, client event invitations, fee disclosure documents, website with clear service descriptions.

Who uses the outputs: CLIENTS interact with almost everything — the onboarding, the plan presentation, the quarterly communications, the website. Prospects evaluate the advisor through these materials.

Community: Very tight. Custodian networks (Schwab, Fidelity, TD) run advisor events. FPA, NAPFA, study groups. Advisors in the same custodian network refer each other to tools constantly.

Money: Advisors managing $50M+ in AUM earn $250K+. One new client worth $500K in assets = $5K/year in recurring fees forever. They'd pay $3-10K for a system that makes them look established. Highest per-engagement revenue potential of any domain on this list.

Why it fits: Every advisor needs the same pieces. The personalization is in their investment philosophy, client niche, and personality. Clients use and see everything. Coherence IS trust — if the website says one thing and the onboarding packet says another, trust is broken. Very tight community. Very high money. Forgiving tolerance (these aren't regulatory filings — they're client-facing materials).

Conference / Event / Retreat Production

What it is: Everything needed to run a professional event — from announcement to post-event follow-up.

The "12 pieces": Event website, speaker/facilitator pages, schedule/program, registration flow (link to Eventbrite), attendee communications (pre/during/post), FAQ / AI attendee assistant ("where do I park?", "which session is best for me?"), sponsor recognition materials, signage copy, slide templates for speakers, feedback survey, post-event summary, photo/recording sharing.

Who uses the outputs: ATTENDEES use everything — the site, the schedule, the communications, the FAQ bot. Sponsors see their recognition materials. Speakers use the templates.

Community: Event organizers tend to run events repeatedly and know each other. Industry conferences, retreat facilitators, summit organizers. Less concentrated than wedding Reddit but still networked.

Money: Event ticket revenue is $50K-$500K+. Current production costs: $20-100K across agencies, designers, and coordinators. A $5-15K coherent package is a fraction of what they'd spend otherwise. And organizers run events REPEATEDLY — same shape, new content each time.

Why it fits: Very repeatable structure. Attendees are the end users of almost everything. Speed matters (event dates are fixed). Coherence is visible to hundreds of people — if the website says one schedule and the printed program says another, it's embarrassing. Imperfection is forgiving — slightly wrong speaker bio isn't a crisis.

Tier 2 — Good fit, some caveats

Therapist / Healer / Coach Practice Setup

What it is: Everything a practitioner needs to go from "I help people" to "I have a functioning practice."

The "12 pieces": Website conveying their approach, intake forms, consent documents, scheduling/booking setup, welcome email sequence, session reminder templates, cancellation/policies, client follow-up communications, Psychology Today profile optimization, content prompts for posts, referral thank-you system, sliding scale / pricing framework.

Who uses the outputs: CLIENTS use almost everything — the website, the intake forms, the communications, the scheduling. Other therapists see the online presence when making referrals.

Community: Very tight. Therapist Facebook groups are massive and recommendation-driven. Training cohorts (every therapist went through a program with peers). Professional associations (APA, NASW). Modality-specific communities (EMDR, somatic, IFS).

Money: Therapists in private practice charge $150-300/session. A full practice = $100-200K/year. But they're often NOT flush when launching — this is the caveat. They'd pay $1,500-3,000 for a complete system, which is real but not premium.

Why it fits: Very repeatable — every therapist needs the same pieces. Personalization is in their approach, modality, and voice. Clients use everything. Tight community. Forgiving tolerance. The caveat is money — they're not wealthy at launch. But the community is so tight that word-of-mouth could compensate.

Course / Workshop Creator Launch

What it is: Everything needed to package and sell a course or workshop — from curriculum to marketing to student experience.

The "12 pieces": Course curriculum/syllabus, landing page copy, email sequences (launch, nurture, onboarding), checkout page, student welcome flow, lesson/module materials, community guidelines, testimonial collection system, content repurposing plan, certificate template, feedback survey, referral/affiliate system.

Who uses the outputs: STUDENTS use the curriculum, the onboarding, the community, the materials. Prospects see the landing page and emails.

Community: Massive. Creator economy communities on Twitter/X, podcasts, Facebook groups. Creators are extremely visible and share what works publicly.

Money: Established creators earning $100K+ will pay $5-15K. But many course creators are early-stage and don't have much yet. The whales pay well; the long tail doesn't.

Why it fits: Repeatable structure — every course launch has the same shape. Students use everything. Huge community with word-of-mouth potential (creators literally have audiences). The caveat is that course CONTENT varies enormously, so the customization goes deeper than weddings. And some creators are sophisticated enough to DIY.

Book Launch System

What it is: Everything an author needs to launch a book — marketing, not writing.

The "12 pieces": Press release, media kit, author bio (multiple lengths), social content calendar, email sequences, book club discussion guide, speaking proposal / one-sheet, launch event plan, review solicitation outreach, retailer/bookstore pitch, podcast guest pitch template, website/landing page.

Who uses the outputs: READERS encounter the marketing. Media contacts use the press kit. Event organizers use the speaking sheet. Bookstores use the pitch.

Community: Very active. Author communities on Facebook, Twitter/X, writing conferences, NaNoWriMo, genre-specific groups. Authors share launch strategies openly.

Money: Variable. Self-published authors may have $1-5K for launch. Traditionally published may have publisher support but want more. Hybrid authors in the middle. $1,500-5,000 price point.

Why it fits: Very repeatable — every book launch is the same shape. The personalization is in the book's content and the author's voice. Tight community. The caveat: lower budget for many authors, and traditionally published authors already have some publisher support.

Independent Consultant Practice Launch

What it is: Expert leaves corporate/firm to start their own consulting practice — needs everything to be a credible independent.

The "12 pieces": Website, service packages with descriptions, proposal template, engagement letter/contract, client onboarding process, project status update template, deliverable templates, invoice system, LinkedIn optimization, thought leadership content plan, referral system, case study framework.

Who uses the outputs: CLIENTS use the website, proposals, onboarding, deliverables, communications.

Community: Moderate. LinkedIn, alumni networks, industry associations. Less concentrated than wedding communities but still networked.

Money: Good. Consultants charge $200-500/hr. They're investing in their livelihood. They'd pay $2-8K. One new client pays for the system many times over.

Why it fits: Fairly repeatable — most consultants need the same infrastructure. Personalization is in their expertise and style. Clients use the outputs. The caveat: consulting businesses ARE more custom than weddings — a strategy consultant's deliverables look different from an HR consultant's. It's less "all the same" than weddings.

Church / Faith Community Operations

What it is: Everything a church or faith community needs to function — member care, events, communication, volunteer coordination.

The "12 pieces": Member communication system (weekly updates, announcements), event coordination templates, volunteer scheduling and onboarding, newcomer welcome process, small group management, giving/donation communication, service/program planning templates, facilities/room booking, pastoral care tracking, seasonal program frameworks, community outreach materials, website content.

Who uses the outputs: CONGREGATION uses everything — communications, event info, volunteer systems, welcome process.

Community: Very tight. Denominational networks are built-in distribution channels. Church planting organizations actively support new churches. Pastors networks.

Money: Variable. Church plants often have planting organization budgets ($50-200K). Established churches have operations budgets. They'd pay $2-5K for a complete system. But many small churches have very little.

Why it fits: Very repeatable — every church needs the same pieces. Congregation uses everything. Denomination networks are excellent channels. The caveat: money is highly variable, and churches can be slow decision-makers.

Eliminated — with reasons

DomainWhy it doesn't pass the wedding test
NonprofitsCan often do it themselves, typically have no money for this, grant requirements vary too much per funder
Construction / GCEvery contractor runs their own process differently — not "all the same shape." Too custom.
E-commerce brandsShopify territory. Too custom, too involved, client IS managing daily. Opposite of "hand it off."
Property flipsEveryone runs their own process. Too custom. No standard "12 pieces."
Creator-to-businessToo much custom. Every creator's business is different. Not repeatable enough.
Estate settlementTolerance for imperfection too low — legal/financial accuracy matters. Harder to reach (no pre-need community).
AdoptionStakes too high. Don't want to mess with legal/placement processes. Tolerance too low.

Wellness retreat launchCan't find them — no concentrated community like Reddit brides. Scattered.

Claude websites08:24
Thinking...

Honestly? I've been through six reports and my own thinking and I want to be real with you: weddings might be genuinely in a league of its own for the complete-process pattern. Let me tell you why nothing else matches, and then the few things that come close that nobody's surfaced yet.

What makes weddings unique is a combination that's really hard to replicate:

  • One-time event — the person will NEVER do this again, so they have zero incentive to learn or DIY. This is what kills Airbnb hosts (they'll learn), course creators (they'll learn), consultants (they'll learn), agents (they'll learn). Brides will never plan another wedding. They just want it done.
  • Budget is pre-allocated — "stationery and digital" is a recognized line item in a $30-50K wedding budget. Nobody questions it. Compared to: try convincing a therapist who just opened a practice to spend $3K on systems.
  • Social pressure — guests are watching, family is judging, it's going on Instagram. This makes people willing to pay for quality in a way that "my consultant website looks okay" never will.
  • Fixed immovable deadline — the wedding date. Can't be pushed. Speed isn't just nice, it's necessary.

The closest structural analogues I haven't seen anyone surface:

Bar/Bat Mitzvahs — Budget $15-50K. Same sub-problems: invitations, RSVP, theme, venue coordination, website, party planning, guest management. One-time per child. Fixed date. Social pressure. Jewish community is tight and recommendation-driven. ~30K bar/bat mitzvahs per year in the US though, so much smaller market.

Quinceañeras — Budget $5-20K. Similar sub-problems. One-time. Fixed date. Latino community is tight, growing, and highly social. Less digitally concentrated (harder to reach through Reddit/forums), but Instagram and family networks are strong.

Fundraising galas — Budget $10-100K for the event. Invitations, event website, silent auction system, donor communications, sponsorship packages, program, seating, follow-up. The ORGANIZER (often a board volunteer) has never done this before and doesn't want to learn. Community: nonprofit world is networked. Repeatable (annual galas). This is essentially "a wedding for organizations."

Beyond that... I think the honest answer is that for the COMPLETE PROCESS pattern, weddings is the play. The other "weddings" are smaller versions of the same thing in other cultural celebration contexts.

For the WEBSITE business (the wish-fulfilling jewel), the domains are broader — independent professionals, architects, therapists, agents — but that's a different shape. One deliverable, not a complete process. And you already identified both of these tracks from the very beginning in the Andrew conversations.

Maybe the question isn't "what's another wedding" but "how deep can we go INTO weddings" — because that's where the complete-process advantage actually exists. And then the website business runs alongside it as the simpler, faster-money track.

What does your felt sense say? Am I finally landing where you already were, or is there still something I'm not seeing?

The add-ons that make it not-commodity:

  • AI chat-against-your-site (built for wedding invitation, works for anything)
  • Airtable CMS for portfolios/dynamic content
  • The retheme superpower (change your branding, we update everything instantly)
  • Monitoring that actually verifies pages work

Now — niches. I think the transcripts reveal something important: the niche isn't an industry. It's an archetype. Jonathan described the person:

  • Has something they want in the world but can't make it
  • Tried tools, everything felt wrong
  • Doesn't want to manage it — wants it DONE
  • Not super picky — wants something beautiful, not pixel-perfect
  • Will pay $1,000-$5,000 for the relief of it being handled

That archetype exists across many industries. The question is: where is the highest concentration of this archetype with real money and tight community?

From the transcripts, the industries that naturally came up:

1. Architecture / design firms — PROVEN. Nabil. High money per client ($5K+ justifiable). Tight community (AIA, design world). Trigger: launching firm, new partner, new portfolio. Standard shape: philosophy + portfolio + project pages. The "why does this matter to you?" question works perfectly because architects HAVE a philosophy.

2. Coaches / therapists / practitioners — Olivia mentioned this repeatedly. Shakafei validated it spontaneously. They're Jonathan's natural network. Tight community (training cohorts, modality groups). Trigger: opening practice, leaving group practice. Standard shape: about + approach + specialties + booking. Money is moderate ($1,500-3,000 realistic) but community word-of-mouth is extremely strong.

3. "Creative professionals who run firms" — Interior designers, landscape architects, boutique branding studios, production companies. They're TASTE people who know what good looks like but can't make their own website feel right. They've tried. They care deeply. They have money (their clients are wealthy). Tight community (designers know designers). This is the Nabil-adjacent world.

4. Professional services going independent — Consultants, lawyers, financial advisors leaving firms. They need credibility fast. They have money (their hourly rate is $200-800). Trigger is clear (going independent). They won't DIY because they're busy getting clients. Standard shape: about + services + case studies + contact. Less felt-sense extraction needed, more speed + quality.

5. Small firm owners whose website embarrasses them — The VC Jonathan mentioned whose website was down during a call. The person who knows their site is bad but hasn't had the bandwidth to fix it. This is cross-industry. They have money. They just need someone to handle it.

What I think is the honest best first move:

Start with #1 and #3 together — design professionals (architects, interior designers, landscape architects, creative firms). This is:

  • Proven (Nabil)
  • High money per client
  • Very tight community (one great site gets shown at every AIA chapter meeting)
  • They CARE about aesthetics but can't make their own site match their standards
  • Standard shape but room for the felt-sense extraction to shine
  • Natural network for Jonathan (already connected through Nabil)

And run #2 (coaches/therapists) alongside as the lower-ticket, higher-volume track through Jonathan's existing community.

What does your felt sense say? Is there something about the design professional world that feels right? Or is there a different concentration of the archetype that I'm not seeing?

KEY INSIGHTS FOR AN AI-POWERED SAME-DAY SERVICE

Biggest opportunities where your service demolishes the status quo:

1. Campaign Landing Pages — The #1 opportunity. Agencies charge $3K-$8K and take 2-4 weeks. A same-day, $200-$500 AI-generated landing page is a category killer. Marketers are the most volume-sensitive buyers.

2. Property Listing Sites — Speed is everything. A luxury listing agent who can have a stunning single-property site live the same day as photos are ready? That's a game-changer vs. waiting a week.

3. Event Sites — Conferences, galas, weddings. All time-sensitive. All follow predictable structures. All are currently overpriced for what they are.

4. Real Estate Agent Sites — New agents, agents switching brokerages, seasonal refreshes. Template-perfect use case with spreadsheet-backed listings.

5. Restaurant Sites — The structure is completely predictable (menu, hours, location, photos). Restaurants hate paying $3K+ for something this simple.

6. Sales Proposal Sites — Emerging category. Sales teams would LOVE to send prospects a custom mini-site instead of a PDF. Speed is the entire value prop.

The sweet spot for your AI service is: pages that are (a) structurally predictable, (b) currently overpriced, (c) time-sensitive, and (d) ordered by non-technical buyers. That's landing pages, real estate, events, restaurants, and professional services — in that order.

Top 15 — Strongest Fit for Our Service

These are ranked by the intersection of: willingness to pay, need for custom, tolerance for good-enough, and speed premium.

#TypeWhy It's the Strongest Fit
1Single property listing siteRealtors routinely pay $500-2K per listing for luxury homes. Every property is unique so templates feel generic. They need it before the listing goes live — days, not weeks. Design just needs to look premium, not win awards. High volume: one agent might buy several per year.
2Wedding websiteEvery wedding is unique (couple's story, venue, details). Couples are time-pressed and overwhelmed. They want something that feels personal, not cookie-cutter Zola. $500-1,500 is trivial relative to wedding budget. Clear scope, clear deadline.
3Conference/summit/event siteOrganizers need it custom (their speakers, their schedule, their brand). Hard deadline (the event date). They have budget. Design just needs to be credible and clear. Annual events rebuy yearly.
4Restaurant siteEvery restaurant has a unique identity/vibe. Owners are busy and non-technical. They need it now (opening soon, or embarrassed by current site). Budget is modest but real ($1-3K). Good-enough is perfect — they just need the menu up and the place to look inviting.
5Memorial / tribute siteExtreme urgency (someone just died). Deeply personal and impossible to template. Families don't care about pixel perfection — they care about dignity. Money is not the concern. Emotionally high-stakes but technically simple.
6Startup / product launch pageFounders need it yesterday — for investor meetings, launches, press. Every startup's story is different. They care about speed and "looks legit" far more than design awards. $500-2K is nothing vs. opportunity cost of delay.
7Small business homepageMassive market. Plumber, dentist, accountant — they need a site. Every business is different enough that templates feel wrong. They want it done, not perfect. Speed matters because they're losing leads daily.
8Political campaign siteElection dates are immovable deadlines. Every candidate is unique. Campaigns have budget and zero time. Design needs to look credible, not award-winning. Seasonal surge during election cycles.
9Professional speaker / author siteSpeakers need a site before the next booking inquiry. Must showcase THEIR talks, THEIR books. They have income and will pay for speed. Good-enough is fine — they just need to look established.
10Course / program landing pageCoaches and educators launching a course need a page that converts. Each course is unique. They have a launch date. They'll pay because the page directly drives revenue.
11Gala / fundraiser event pageFixed dates, dedicated committees with budget, need custom content (honorees, sponsors). Good-enough is expected — it's a nonprofit. Speed is essential as events approach.
12Medical / dental practice sitePractices lose patients daily without a good site. Every practice is different. They have revenue and will pay. They care about "professional and trustworthy," not bleeding-edge design.
13New development / real estate project siteDevelopers have serious budget. Each project is unique. They need it before sales launch. "Looks premium" is enough — buyers visit the model home for the real experience.
14Proposal / pitch site — custom site to pitch a specific clientAgencies will pay $1-5K to win a $100K+ contract. Every pitch is bespoke. Deadline is the pitch date. Content matters more than pixel perfection.
15Nonprofit homepageNonprofits need to look credible to donors and grantmakers. Each org's mission is unique. Limited budget but will pay $1-3K. Good-enough is fine — donors care about mission, not design trends.

Key Patterns Across the Top 15

  • Hard deadlines drive urgency (events, launches, listings, elections, deaths)
  • Revenue-connected pages justify the spend (the page directly enables income or fundraising)
  • Inherently unique content makes templates inadequate (every property, couple, candidate is different)
  • Non-designer buyers have calibrated expectations — they want "professional," not "perfect"
  • Repeat/volume potential in several categories (realtors buy per listing, events recur annually, agencies pitch repeatedly)

Trigger Events That Create Urgent Demand for a Custom Website

I brainstormed 60 trigger events across life, business, community, and professional domains, then ranked the top 15. Here's the full analysis.

LIFE EVENTS — Personal

1. Someone Dies

  • Page needed: Memorial / obituary / celebration of life page
  • Speed: Same day to 48 hours
  • Who pays: Family member, typically 30-60 age range
  • Price tolerance: $50–$200
  • Template vs custom: Custom matters — it needs to feel like this person

2. Engagement

  • Page needed: Wedding website (save the date, venue info, registry links, RSVP)
  • Speed: Within a week, but often procrastinated then suddenly urgent
  • Who pays: Couple, or parents
  • Price tolerance: $50–$150
  • Template vs custom: Custom preferred — couples want "their" aesthetic

3. Baby Born / Baby Announcement

  • Page needed: Birth announcement page, photo gallery, gift registry link
  • Speed: 1–3 days
  • Who pays: Parents
  • Price tolerance: $25–$75
  • Template vs custom: Template okay, but personal touches matter

4. Milestone Birthday (50th, 80th, etc.)

  • Page needed: Tribute page with photos, timeline, guestbook for messages
  • Speed: 1–2 weeks before party
  • Who pays: Family member organizing the party
  • Price tolerance: $30–$100
  • Template vs custom: Custom preferred

5. Retirement Party

  • Page needed: Tribute page, career timeline, message board for colleagues
  • Speed: 1–2 weeks before event
  • Who pays: Organizing colleague or family
  • Price tolerance: $30–$100
  • Template vs custom: Template okay

6. Graduation

  • Page needed: Celebration page, photo gallery, next-chapter announcement
  • Speed: Days before ceremony
  • Who pays: Parents or grad
  • Price tolerance: $25–$75
  • Template vs custom: Template okay

  • Who pays: The individual
  • Price tolerance: $50–$150

TOP 15 TRIGGERS — Ranked by Urgency + Willingness to Pay + AI Same-Day Advantage

Tier 1 — Strongest Opportunities

1. Someone Dies — Memorial Page

Urgency: Extreme (same day). No one plans for this. Funeral homes charge $200+ for basic obituary pages. Current alternatives feel generic and impersonal. A custom memorial page that captures who this person was, built from a conversation while grief is raw, is deeply valuable. Price: $75–$200. Volume: ~3M deaths/year in the US alone.

2. Just Started a Business — Need a Website Today

Urgency: High. They filed an LLC, they're telling people, and the first question is "what's your website?" Every day without a site is lost credibility. Current path: Squarespace takes days of learning, hiring a designer takes weeks and $2K+. Same-day custom site from a conversation is a game-changer. Price: $100–$300. Massive volume.

3. Big Client Meeting / Pitch Tomorrow — Need to Look Legit

Urgency: Extreme (tonight). Consultants, freelancers, and small agencies scramble to build credibility before a make-or-break meeting. The ROI is enormous — a $200 site could win a $20K contract. Nothing else can produce a custom, professional site overnight. Price: $100–$300.

4. Product Launch on a Deadline

Urgency: High (date-locked). Startups, indie makers, and small companies constantly need launch pages. The launch date doesn't move. Current path: hire a freelancer (weeks, $1K+) or DIY (days). Same-day from a conversation is a clear win. Price: $100–$500.

5. Freelancer / Consultant Needs a Portfolio NOW

Urgency: High. Triggered by "can you send me your portfolio?" or a job application. The opportunity has a shelf life of hours. Price: $50–$200. Very large addressable market.

Tier 2 — Strong Opportunities

6. Running for Local Office

Urgency: High (filing deadline triggers it). Thousands of local races annually. Candidates are not tech people. A campaign page with platform, bio, donate link — built from one conversation — is dramatically better than what most local candidates have. Price: $100–$300.

7. Crisis Communication / PR Response

Urgency: Extreme (hours). When something goes wrong publicly, companies need a dedicated response page fast. Agencies charge thousands and take days. Price: $200–$500. Lower volume but very high willingness to pay.

8. Fundraiser for a Crisis (Medical Emergency, Disaster)

Urgency: Extreme (same day). GoFundMe is limited in storytelling. A companion page with the full story creates trust and drives more donations. Price: $50–$150.

9. Wedding Website

Urgency: Medium-high (procrastinated then suddenly urgent). Huge market. Zola/The Knot exist but are generic. Couples want their aesthetic. Price: $50–$150. Enormous volume.

10. Event Landing Page (Conference, Workshop, Community Event)

Urgency: High (date-locked). Event organizers always underestimate how long the website takes. A custom event page with the right vibe, built same-day, solves a real pain point. Price: $100–$300.

Tier 3 — Good Opportunities

11. Selling a House FSBO

Urgency: High. Homeowners saving $15K+ in agent commissions will gladly spend $100–$200 on a beautiful single-property page. Price: $100–$200.

12. Author / Book Launch

Urgency: Medium-high (pub date is fixed). Authors are often not technical. A custom page matching the book's aesthetic is exactly right. Price: $75–$250.

13. Speaker Page ("Send Me Your Speaker Page")

Urgency: High (immediate ask). Not having one loses the gig. Price: $75–$200.

14. Course / Workshop Launch

Urgency: Medium-high (enrollment deadline). Creators need sales pages but current path is Kajabi ($150/mo) or hire a designer ($1K+). Price: $75–$250.

15. Nonprofit Just Incorporated

Urgency: High. Need to start fundraising immediately. Donors expect a website. Price: $75–$250.

Why These Top 15 Win

The common thread:

1. The trigger is external and the deadline is real — something happened that created urgency

2. The person who needs it is not a web designer — they're grieving, launching, scrambling

3. Custom matters but perfection doesn't — "professional and personal" is the bar

4. Current alternatives are too slow, too expensive, or too generic — this service fills a gap nothing else does

5. Same-day delivery is a genuine differentiator — the value of the site decays rapidly with time. Speed is the product.

All three back. Here's what jumps out when I cross them:

The single strongest new finding: property listing sites for real estate agents.

All three agents found this independently and ranked it highly. Here's why it's interesting:

  • An agent lists a luxury home and needs a standalone site for that specific property — photos, story of the home, neighborhood, virtual tour embed, agent contact
  • Same shape EVERY time (like wedding invitations — all different, all the same structure)
  • Speed is CRITICAL — listing goes live, they need the site NOW
  • One agent = many listings = recurring revenue. An active luxury agent might buy 10-20 per year
  • Price: $500-$3,000 per property site. Trivial when selling a $1M+ home
  • Good-enough is PERFECT — buyers visit the house for the real experience. The site just needs to look premium and have the right info
  • Nobody needs to DIY this — agents want it done
  • The AI chat feature works here too: "Ask about this property" — schools, commute, neighborhood

This has a very different feel than "become a real estate agent's full digital system" (which felt like entering a whole ecosystem). This is narrow: one site per property, delivered same-day, recurring per agent. Like wedding invitations but for houses.

Other types that converged across all three that feel worth noting:

  • Restaurant sites — structurally predictable (menu, hours, vibe, location, reservation link), owners are busy and non-technical, every restaurant is unique enough that templates feel wrong, $1,500-$3,000
  • Startup/product launch pages — deadline-driven, founders need it yesterday, clear ROI ("looks legit" for investor meetings), $500-$2,000
  • Conference/event sites — deadline-driven, repeating annually, $3,000-$10,000
  • "I need to look legit by tomorrow" emergency sites — consultant has a pitch meeting, freelancer got asked "what's your website?", business just incorporated. Extreme urgency. The overnight custom site as a product.

The trigger-events agent surfaced something interesting: a lot of the highest-urgency demand comes from moments where speed IS the entire product. Not "I should probably get a website someday" but "I need this by TUESDAY." That's a different positioning than "we do beautiful custom work." It's "we deliver custom overnight."

What catches your eye here? The property listing site thing feels alive to me — it's narrow, repeatable, high-money, and structurally very similar to what you already proved you can do.

Completed fresh site types analysis, 52 types with top 10

Done. Wrote the analysis to /Users/j/Dropbox/_Everything/Pinned/2026-03-19 Websites/Output/fresh-site-types-1.md.

52 website types total, each with what/who/why/repeatable analysis. Top 10 highlights:

1. Funeral/memorial tribute pages — 2.8M deaths/yr in US, extreme urgency, identical structure, funeral home partnership potential

2. Local political campaign sites — predictable election cycle demand, campaign funds ready to spend, completely standardized

3. Startup pitch/investor landing pages — founders raising money are the least price-sensitive buyers; ROI is obvious

4. Event venue/banquet hall sites — $5K-$50K bookings, most venues have terrible sites, huge addressable market

5. Construction/trades/contractor sites — millions of trade businesses, terrible websites, single lead pays for the site

6. Retreat/workshop/intensive sites — recurring revenue (same client, new retreat each season)

7. Proposal/pitch microsites — agencies pitching $100K+ deals, retainer-style relationship

8. Franchise location pages — 50 locations = 50 identical sites, pure scaling economics

9. Conference/summit sites — large pre-allocated budgets, annual recurrence, immovable deadlines

10. Daycare/preschool sites — obsessive parent researchers, one enrolled child = $12K-$24K/yr revenue

Key patterns identified: urgency-driven sites command premiums (your same-day differentiator), high-ticket businesses pay without flinching, volume plays with identical structure are most operationally efficient, and some niches offer recurring revenue from the same client.

Thinking...

Two reports fully read, third still writing. But I have enough to tell you what I actually think.

Across 150+ website types from all these reports, here's what jumps out as genuinely new and interesting — things we HAVEN'T already been discussing:

Event venues (wedding venues, banquet halls, conference centers) — This one keeps surfacing and I think it's real. Bookings are $5K-$50K each. Most venue sites are embarrassingly bad. The structure is completely predictable (spaces, capacity, packages, gallery, inquiry form). And here's the kicker: it connects to the wedding business. Venue → refers couples → your wedding stationery/website service. It's a lead channel AND a revenue stream. And the AI feature works perfectly here: "Ask about this venue" — capacity, catering options, availability, what's included.

Daycare/preschool sites — Parents research obsessively. One enrolled child = $12K-$24K/year in revenue. There are 500K+ childcare businesses in the US. Most have terrible websites. The structure is completely standardized. These owners are too busy running the daycare to deal with websites. This is a surprisingly big, badly underserved market.

Tour operators / experience companies — Charter fishing, walking tours, adventure guides, cooking classes. Tourists book online. Very similar to Airbnb structurally (photos, description, what to expect, booking link) but for experiences. The AI chat feature is perfect: "what should I wear?", "is it family-friendly?", "what's the cancellation policy?"

Executive/career transition sites — C-suite people leaving a role, becoming board members, advisors, speakers. They have MONEY ($50K-$300K board seats). They have NO time. They need to look established yesterday. Very standard structure. This is a small niche but very high ticket.

Proposal/pitch microsites — Agencies sending a custom site instead of a PDF to pitch a $100K+ deal. Recurring (same agency, many pitches). Clear ROI. But this is very B2B and might not be where you want to play.

Beyond these, honestly? Most of what came up is variations on what we already know — professional services sites, trades/contractors, photographers, etc. The landscape of "types of websites people pay for" is actually not that surprising once you've seen it. The money is in the niches we've already identified plus these few new ones.

The thing that keeps standing out to me: event venues feels like the most interesting new addition, specifically because of the wedding connection. What do you think?

56 site types written, sharing top 10 and key patterns

Done. Written to /Users/j/Dropbox/_Everything/Pinned/2026-03-19 Websites/Output/fresh-site-types-2.md.

56 site types total, organized into 12 categories. Here are the highlights:

Top 10 most interesting opportunities (ranked):

1. Memorial / tribute sites — Emotional urgency (funeral in 3 days), no incumbent, high willingness to pay, same-day delivery is perfectly matched. AI chat could let guests explore someone's life story.

2. Conference / summit / retreat sites — Hard deadline, annual repeat client, Airtable-backed dynamic content is killer for changing speaker lineups. Conference organizers are a tight referral network.

3. General contractor portfolio sites — Massive underserved market (hundreds of thousands of contractors with terrible sites making $200K-$1M+). Before/after galleries are a natural fit. Contractors know contractors.

4. Nonprofit annual report pages — Built-in annual repeat revenue. Consistent format. Replaces $5K-$15K printed reports. AI chat lets donors query specific impact data.

5. Fundraiser / gala event pages — Hard deadline, clear ROI ($2K site for a $200K gala), annual repeat, development directors are deeply networked.

6. Senior living facility sites — $36K-$120K/year per resident LTV. Adult children researching in crisis need warmth and answers. AI chat is extraordinary here ("Does your facility accept Medicaid?" "Can mom bring her cat?").

7. Private school / micro-school sites — $10K-$50K/year tuition, philosophy-forward schools map perfectly to "carrying forward the philosophy." Micro-school movement is growing fast.

8. Pet breeder sites — Non-obvious, surprisingly high revenue ($10K-$25K per litter), clear legitimacy signaling need, breed communities are tight referral networks.

9. Winery / brewery / distillery sites — 22,000+ in the US, care about aesthetics, Airtable perfect for rotating tap lists/wine releases, tight regional networks.

10. Tattoo artist portfolio sites — Artists making $100K-$300K managing everything through Instagram DMs. Style-filtered portfolio + booking system transforms their workflow. Artist communities spread the word fast.

Key patterns I identified:

  • Same-day delivery creates most value for: memorials, conferences, campaigns, product launches, startup landing pages
  • AI chat creates most differentiation for: senior living, medical practices, memorials, schools, nonprofits
  • Airtable dynamic content is the killer feature for: conferences, wineries/breweries, real estate agents, podcasts, contractor portfolios
  • Best referral network categories: contractors, breeders, wineries, school founders, nonprofit development directors, tattoo artists

Claude websites08:56
Thinking...

OK, here's my actual thinking. Not synthesizing agents — thinking for myself.

What I keep coming back to:

The niche question is really a marketing question. The SERVICE is the same regardless of who you serve — you sit with someone, get what they need, AI produces it, they have a site by end of day. The niche determines where you FIND clients and how you TALK to them.

So the real question is: where can you build a flywheel fastest? Flywheel = get a client → deliver → they love it → they tell people like them → more clients come.

What makes a flywheel spin fast:

  • Tight community (one happy client reaches many potential clients)
  • Clear trigger event (people actively searching at a specific moment)
  • Visual result (people can SEE the work and share it)
  • Recurring potential (same client or same community keeps buying)

Where I'd actually put money if it were my business:

Real estate property listing pages is the one I can't stop thinking about. Here's why: one agent is an entire BOOK of business. You close one agent, and they bring you 10-20 properties per year. The agent shows the property page to the seller ("look what we do for our listings"), and the seller is impressed. The agent shows it to other agents at the brokerage ("look at this"), and they want the same thing. One brokerage with 200 agents is a pipeline that feeds you for years. The per-site cost is lower ($500-$1,500) but the volume and recurring nature makes it a real business. And the speed advantage is EXTREME — the listing goes live tomorrow, they need the site today. Nobody else delivers same-day custom property pages.

This is also the niche where AI quality is MOST appropriate. Nobody expects a property page to be a design masterpiece. They expect it to look premium, show the photos well, convey the lifestyle, and have the right info. That's exactly what AI delivers well.

Architecture/design firms is the proven case but it's a smaller market. There are maybe 20,000 architecture firms in the US. It's the credibility play — the case study, the portfolio piece, the "look what we can do." Start here because you already have it.

Therapists/coaches is the natural network play. Jonathan is literally surrounded by these people. Olivia, Shakafei, Eva. The community spreads recommendations like fire. Lower ticket but the volume is huge and the word-of-mouth is the strongest of any niche on this list. And this is where the felt-sense extraction is most appreciated — therapists understand deep listening because it's what THEY do.

Contractors/trades is the sleeping giant. Millions of businesses, terrible websites, one lead pays for the site 10x over. But Jonathan isn't naturally connected to this world, and they're harder to reach. This is where the SEO play becomes critical — "contractor website" searches have massive volume. This might be the play once the SEO machine is built.

Event venues is the strategic play that connects to weddings. Venue site → venue refers couples → wedding stationery/website. It's both revenue and a lead channel for the wedding track.

The SEO insight that I think is genuinely high leverage:

Don't just target "[niche] website." Target the PROBLEMS. The same play you discussed for weddings:

  • "Why doesn't my therapy website get clients"
  • "How to make my contractor website look professional"
  • "Best way to showcase real estate listings online"
  • "My restaurant website doesn't match our vibe"

Each problem page has: the answer to the problem + a live example built with AI + a CTA to the service. You build these for EVERY niche simultaneously. The pages themselves demonstrate the service. And you can auto-generate hundreds of them because... you have AI.

That's the thing nobody else can do. Squarespace can't generate 500 niche-specific example sites. An agency can't. But you can produce a live, beautiful example for every single niche as proof of concept, and each one becomes an SEO magnet.

If I had to pick the order:

1. Architecture firms — you have the case study, start here for credibility

2. Therapists/coaches — natural network, instant word-of-mouth

3. Real estate property pages — productize this as a specific offering, approach brokerages

4. Start building SEO pages across ALL niches simultaneously (this is a teammate job)

5. Event venues — when the wedding track gets going

What's your felt sense on this? Does the real estate property page thing resonate, or does something else pull you more?

Done. Here's what I found and built:

JSONL Structure:

  • 528 lines total, each a JSON object with a type field
  • Key types: user, assistant, file-history-snapshot, progress
  • Messages have message.role and message.content (string or array of content blocks)
  • Content block types: text, tool_use, tool_result, thinking (with opaque encrypted signatures)
  • Tool uses contain name and input fields
  • Tool results come as user type messages with tool_result content blocks
  • Teammate messages appear as <teammate-message> XML tags inside user content
  • teamName field indicates team context (e.g., "websites")
  • toolUseResult field on tool result entries has richer data (stdout, stderr, file diffs, etc.)
  • isApiErrorMessage, isMeta flags for special message types

Script: /Users/j/Dropbox/_Everything/Pinned/2026-03-19 Websites/scripts/transcript-to-html.py

  • Accepts JSONL path as CLI argument, optional output path as second argument
  • Reusable for any Claude Code conversation transcript
